<a id="password-based-authentication"></a>

**Security assessment**

_Not assessed in detail; see the control overview in [§7.1](#71-security-control-overview)._

**Relevant findings**

- None identified for this control.
#### 7.2.2 Password-Based Authentication

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - the login route concatenates `req.body.email` into raw SQL, allowing authentication bypass without a credential, and passwords are hashed with unsalted `MD5`.

`routes/login.ts` handles `POST /rest/user/login` by comparing a submitted password against the `Users` table. The password is hashed via `security.hash()` (`MD5`, `lib/insecurity.ts:41`) before comparison. Hardcoded challenge credentials also live in the login route (`routes/login.ts:60-65`). No account lockout or progressive delay is applied to the login endpoint at `server.ts:596`.

The diagram shows the intended password login path:

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    autonumber
    actor User
    participant SPA as Angular SPA
    participant API as Login Route (routes/login.ts)
    participant DB as SQLite Users table

    User->>SPA: Enter email + password
    SPA->>API: POST /rest/user/login
    API->>API: MD5-hash password (lib/insecurity.ts:41)
    API->>DB: SELECT * FROM Users WHERE email='...' AND password='...'
    DB-->>API: First matching row
    API-->>SPA: JWT + basket id
```

**Security assessment**

Two independent weaknesses sit on the login path:

- `routes/login.ts:34` interpolates `req.body.email` into a raw SQL template literal via `models.sequelize.query()`. Submitting `' OR true--` comments out the password clause and returns the first `Users` row (the seeded admin account), granting admin access with no credential.
- `lib/insecurity.ts:41` hashes passwords with unsalted `MD5` (`crypto.createHash('md5')`). A dump of the Users table obtained through the SQL injection immediately yields crackable hashes via lookup tables.

No per-account brute-force protection exists on the login endpoint (`server.ts:596`); the rate limiter at `server.ts:343` applies only to the password-reset route.

The raw SQL construction at the login route:

```ts
models.sequelize.query(
  `SELECT * FROM Users WHERE email = '${req.body.email || ''}' AND password = '${security.hash(req.body.password || '')}' AND deletedAt IS NULL`
)
```

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-005](#f-005) — OAuth implicit flow creates a guessable parallel credential derived from the email address, bypassing the password control entirely.
- 🔴 [F-008](#f-008) — Insecure JWT verification (no algorithm pin) allows token forgery even when the password control holds.
- 🟠 [F-016](#f-016) — The OAuth-derived password (`btoa(reversed email)`) is also accepted by the normal login endpoint, defeating password-based authentication for all OAuth-created accounts.

<a id="google-oauth-20"></a>
#### 7.2.3 Google OAuth 2.0

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - OAuth is implemented as a frontend implicit-flow adapter that terminates in a locally-derived guessable password rather than a server-side authorization-code exchange.

`oauth.component.ts` implements OAuth entirely in the Angular frontend. The component reads the access token from the redirect URL fragment, calls Google's userinfo endpoint to retrieve the profile email, derives a local password as `btoa(email.split('').reverse().join(''))`, and then calls the standard local `POST /rest/user/login` endpoint. Google OAuth client configuration lives in `config/default.yml` (`clientId`, `authorizedRedirects`).

The diagram shows how the OAuth adapter ultimately enters the local login flow:

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    autonumber
    actor User
    participant SPA as OAuth Component (oauth.component.ts)
    participant Google as Google UserInfo API
    participant API as Local Login Route
    participant JWT as JWT Issuer (lib/insecurity.ts)

    User->>SPA: Return from OAuth redirect (access_token in URL fragment)
    SPA->>Google: GET userinfo with access_token
    Google-->>SPA: Email address
    SPA->>API: POST /rest/user/login (email + btoa(reversed email))
    API->>JWT: Issue local session JWT on successful match
    API-->>SPA: JWT + basket id
```

**Security assessment**

This is a frontend identity adapter, not a server-side OAuth/OIDC authorization-code flow. The derived password (`btoa(email.split('').reverse().join(''))` at `oauth.component.ts:30`) is a pure function of the victim's public email address - anyone who knows the email can compute it and authenticate through the standard login endpoint without an OAuth redirect. No PKCE, `state`, or `nonce` validation is performed on the redirect (`oauth.component.ts:70-79`).

```ts
// oauth.component.ts:30
const password = btoa(profile.email.split('').reverse().join(''))
```

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-005](#f-005) — The implicit flow with email-derived password creates a universally guessable credential for every Google-linked account.
- 🔴 [F-008](#f-008) — Insecure JWT verification means a forged token can bypass even a corrected OAuth flow.
- 🟠 [F-016](#f-016) — `routes/login.ts:64` accepts the same email-derived password in the standard password-login path, confirming the bypass is not limited to the OAuth redirect.

<a id="totp-2fa-multi-factor-authentication"></a><a id="two-factor-authentication"></a><a id="two-factor-authentication-totp"></a>
#### 7.2.4 TOTP/2FA (Multi-Factor Authentication)

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - TOTP enrollment and verification are implemented but the TOTP secret is stored in plaintext, 2FA is not enforced for privileged accounts, and seed data commits TOTP secrets in cleartext.

`routes/2fa.ts` implements TOTP enrollment (`POST /rest/2fa/setup`) and verification (`POST /rest/2fa/verify`) using the speakeasy library. A user who completes enrollment stores a `totpSecret` column on their `Users` row (`models/user.ts:112`). The verification route checks the submitted TOTP token against the stored secret and issues a JWT on success.

The diagram shows the intended TOTP verification path:

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    autonumber
    actor User
    participant SPA as 2FA Entry Component
    participant API as 2FA Verify Route (routes/2fa.ts)
    participant DB as Users table (models/user.ts)
    participant TOTP as speakeasy TOTP library

    User->>SPA: Enter 6-digit TOTP code
    SPA->>API: POST /rest/2fa/verify {token, tempToken}
    API->>DB: SELECT totpSecret WHERE id = decoded.id
    DB-->>API: plaintext totpSecret
    API->>TOTP: speakeasy.totp.verify({secret, token})
    TOTP-->>API: valid / invalid
    API-->>SPA: JWT on success
```

**Security assessment**

TOTP is implemented as an opt-in second factor only; it is not enforced for admin or privileged accounts. Two storage weaknesses undermine the possession factor:

- `models/user.ts:112` stores `totpSecret` as a plain VARCHAR column with no encryption. A SQL injection or database dump yields TOTP secrets directly.
- `data/static/users.yml:3` commits cleartext TOTP seeds alongside plaintext passwords for seeded accounts, so the repository itself is a TOTP oracle for every pre-seeded user.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-005](#f-005) — The OAuth-derived password bypass effectively circumvents 2FA for Google-linked accounts because the attacker never triggers the TOTP step.
- 🔴 [F-008](#f-008) — Insecure JWT verification allows an attacker with a forged token to skip the 2FA gate entirely.
- 🟠 [F-016](#f-016) — The email-derived password used by the OAuth adapter enters via the same login endpoint and does not require a second factor.

### 7.3 Session and Token Controls

**Verdict:** 🔴 Unsafe

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.3.1 JWT Session Tokens](#731-jwt-session-tokens)
- [7.3.2 CSRF Protection](#732-csrf-protection)

**Implemented controls:** `lib/insecurity.ts:21,54,55`; `jsonwebtoken@0.4.0`; `jws` library; None (for CSRF).

**Assessment:** This application uses a single locally-signed token format (commonly called JWT) for every authenticated session, regardless of the login flow in [§7.2](#72-identity-and-authentication-controls) that established it. The sub-sections below trace one token through its lifecycle: signing on issuance, validation on every protected request, and storage in the browser. The signing key is committed to the public repository (`lib/insecurity.ts:21`), the verifier accepts any algorithm declared in the token header, and the token is stored in `localStorage` - all three lifecycle stages are simultaneously defeated. CSRF protection is absent (`server.ts:183`).

The diagram shows the full session token flow from issuance to browser presentation:

```mermaid
sequenceDiagram
    autonumber
    actor User
    participant SPA as Angular SPA
    participant API as Express Backend
    participant LS as localStorage
    participant Key as Hardcoded RSA Key (lib/insecurity.ts:21)

    User->>API: Successful login (any method)
    API->>Key: Load private key literal
    Key-->>API: PEM string
    API->>API: jwt.sign(payload, privateKey, {algorithm: 'RS256'}) → lib/insecurity.ts:54
    API-->>SPA: { token, basket_id }
    SPA->>LS: localStorage.setItem('token', token)
    Note over LS: Readable by any same-origin script
    User->>SPA: Navigate to protected route
    SPA->>LS: localStorage.getItem('token')
    SPA->>API: GET /api/... Authorization: Bearer token
    API->>API: jws.verify(token, publicKey) - no algorithm pin (lib/insecurity.ts:55)
    API-->>SPA: Protected resource
```

<a id="jwt-session-tokens"></a><a id="jwt-session-tokens-rs256"></a>
#### 7.3.1 JWT Session Tokens

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - the signing key is hardcoded in the public repository, the verifier accepts any algorithm the token declares, and the token is stored in `localStorage` where XSS can exfiltrate it.

⚠ **Anti-pattern:** JWT in localStorage

`RS256`-signed JWTs are issued by `lib/insecurity.ts:54` (`jwt.sign(user, privateKey, {algorithm: 'RS256'})`) after every successful authentication. The private key is a string literal at `lib/insecurity.ts:21`. The corresponding public key is `encryptionkeys/jwt.pub`. `verify()` at `lib/insecurity.ts:55` calls `jws.verify(token, publicKey)` without pinning any algorithm. The Angular SPA writes the returned token to `localStorage.setItem('token', ...)` in four auth flows and reads it back for every subsequent API call (`Authorization: Bearer` header).

**Security assessment**

Three independent weaknesses defeat the session token control:

- The RSA private key at `lib/insecurity.ts:21` is a committed string literal in the public repository. Any reader of the source can mint a token the server accepts for any user, including `role=admin`.
- `lib/insecurity.ts:55` calls `jws.verify(token, publicKey)` with no `algorithms` restriction. With `jsonwebtoken@0.4.0`, a token with `alg:none` in the header passes verification without any signature.
- `localStorage` token storage (`oauth.component.ts:51`, `login.component.ts:101`, `two-factor-auth-enter.component.ts:53`, `payment.component.ts:232`) makes the session credential readable by any same-origin script. An XSS payload executes `localStorage.getItem('token')` and exfiltrates a still-valid credential.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-001](#f-001) — JWT stored in `localStorage` across four auth flows; any XSS exfiltrates a full, valid session credential with no further interaction.

<a id="csrf-protection"></a>
#### 7.3.2 CSRF Protection

**Status:** 🔴 Missing - no CSRF token, no `SameSite` cookie attribute, and no origin-header check protect state-changing endpoints.

State-changing REST endpoints (POST, PUT, DELETE) at `server.ts:183` are configured with `app.options('*', cors())` and no CSRF middleware. Because the session credential is a `localStorage`-read Bearer token (not a cookie), the primary CSRF vector is reduced - but any route that additionally accepts cookie-based authentication (or a future migration to `HttpOnly` cookies without a CSRF layer) would be immediately vulnerable. No `csurf` middleware or equivalent origin-check is applied.

**Security assessment**

The absence of CSRF protection is currently partially mitigated by Bearer-token authentication (a cross-origin request cannot read `localStorage`), but this is an architectural fragility: moving to `HttpOnly` cookies (the correct fix for the `localStorage` issue) without simultaneously adding CSRF protection reintroduces the full CSRF attack surface. Both controls must be added together.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-001](#f-001) — The `localStorage` JWT pattern is the underlying reason CSRF is not yet the primary attack; fixing it without CSRF mitigation closes one hole while opening another.

### 7.4 Authorization Controls

**Verdict:** 🔴 Unsafe

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.4.1 Role-Based Access Control](#741-role-based-access-control)
- [7.4.2 Object-Level Authorization](#742-object-level-authorization)

**Implemented controls:** `lib/insecurity.ts:156-171`; `express-jwt` middleware; JWT role claims; Partial checks in some routes; no systematic BOLA framework.

**Assessment:** Role-based access control exists as JWT claim inspection in `lib/insecurity.ts:156-171`, but the role claim is read from the client-supplied token - which can be forged using the committed signing key ([§7.3](#73-session-and-token-controls)). Object-level authorization is applied inconsistently: many routes read `req.user.id` from the token but several substitutable parameters (`userId`, `UserId`, `ownerId`) accept request-body values and bypass ownership checks. The Angular frontend enforces admin access via client-side guard only (`app.guard.ts:54`), providing no actual server-side protection.

<a id="role-based-access-control"></a>
#### 7.4.1 Role-Based Access Control

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - role claims are read from a JWT signed with a committed key, and the admin route guard is client-side only.

`lib/insecurity.ts:156-171` implements `isAuthorized()` and `isAccounting()` as Express middleware that reads the `role` claim from the verified JWT. `express-jwt` validates the signature on each request. The Angular `AdminGuard` at `frontend/src/app/app.guard.ts:54` additionally gates admin route rendering by checking `payload.data.role === roles.admin` in the client-decoded token.

**Security assessment**

The RBAC control depends on JWT signature integrity, which is defeated at two independent points: the signing key is committed (`lib/insecurity.ts:21`) and the verifier accepts `alg:none` (`lib/insecurity.ts:55`). Either route yields a valid `role=admin` token. `AdminGuard` at `app.guard.ts:54` decodes the JWT on the client side - it controls only which Angular component renders, not what API calls succeed. An attacker bypasses the SPA entirely and calls admin REST endpoints directly.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-002](#f-002) — `AdminGuard` authorizes from a client-decoded JWT claim; backend route protection must not rely on this guard.
- 🟠 [F-004](#f-004) — SQL injection on the login route issues a valid JWT without any role forging, granting admin access before RBAC is evaluated.
- 🔴 [F-010](#f-010) — Forged JWT with `role=admin` satisfies `isAuthorized()` and `isAccounting()` middleware because the signing key is public.

<a id="object-level-authorization"></a><a id="object-level-authorization-bolaidor"></a>
#### 7.4.2 Object-Level Authorization

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - ownership checks are applied inconsistently; multiple routes accept caller-supplied object identifiers from the request body rather than from the verified token.

Most Sequelize-backed routes read the authenticated user's id from `req.user.id` (derived from the verified JWT). However, basket-item, address, payment, delivery, deluxe, order, order-history, and wallet routes across `routes/basketItems.ts`, `routes/address.ts`, `routes/payment.ts`, `routes/delivery.ts`, `routes/deluxe.ts`, `routes/order.ts`, `routes/orderHistory.ts`, and `routes/wallet.ts` reference `req.body.UserId` or `req.body.userId` for WHERE clauses, allowing any authenticated user to substitute another user's id and access or modify that user's records.

**Security assessment**

The inconsistency is structural: routes that use `req.user.id` (from the JWT) are safe; routes that accept `req.body.*Id` are systematically bypassed. There is no central ownership-enforcement layer - each route implements (or omits) the check independently, and the majority of the 16 BOLA-affected instances omit it.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-002](#f-002) — Client-side admin guard bypassed; backend authorization is the only real enforcement layer.
- 🟠 [F-004](#f-004) — Login SQL injection yields a JWT for the admin user, after which all object-level checks are trivially bypassed using the admin identity.
- 🔴 [F-010](#f-010) — Forgeable JWT role claim means an attacker can present any user id without triggering an ownership mismatch.

### 7.5 Query Construction and Data Access Controls

**Verdict:** 🔴 Unsafe

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.5.1 SQL Injection Prevention](#751-sql-injection-prevention)

**Implemented controls:** Sequelize 6 ORM (partial); raw `sequelize.query()` calls in `login.ts:34`, `search.ts:47`.

**Assessment:** Most data access goes through Sequelize model APIs that generate parameterized queries by default. Two routes deviate: `routes/login.ts:34` and `routes/search.ts:47` construct raw SQL strings by interpolating request parameters directly. A MarsDB selector in the chatbot review tool additionally accepts user-controlled structure. The two raw-SQL call sites are on the highest-traffic, pre-authentication paths (login and search), maximizing exploitation exposure.

<a id="sql-injection-prevention"></a>
#### 7.5.1 SQL Injection Prevention

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - two authentication and search routes bypass the ORM and interpolate user input directly into SQL strings.

Sequelize models back most relational data access. Two routes call `models.sequelize.query()` with template-literal interpolation instead of bind parameters: `routes/login.ts:34` (email and password) and `routes/search.ts:47` (search criteria). A chatbot review lookup at `routes/chat.ts:149` uses a `$where` JavaScript predicate string that concatenates user input into a MarsDB selector.

The login route illustrates the raw SQL construction pattern that bypasses parameterization:

```ts
models.sequelize.query(
  `SELECT * FROM Users WHERE email = '${req.body.email || ''}' AND password = '${security.hash(req.body.password || '')}' AND deletedAt IS NULL`
)
```

**Security assessment**

The login injection (`routes/login.ts:34`) is pre-authentication; submitting `' OR true--` as the email short-circuits the WHERE clause and returns the first Users row (the seeded admin account). The search injection (`routes/search.ts:47`) is post-authentication and enables UNION-style data exfiltration across all SQLite tables. The MarsDB `$where` injection (`routes/chat.ts:149`) allows operator injection into the in-memory product-review database.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-006](#f-006) — SQL injection in the login query (`routes/login.ts:34`) grants admin access without any credential.
- 🔴 [F-011](#f-011) — SQL injection in the product search route (`routes/search.ts:47`) enables UNION data exfiltration.
- 🟠 [F-029](#f-029) — NoSQL injection via `$where` string concatenation in the chatbot review lookup (`routes/chat.ts:149`).

### 7.6 Input Boundary Validation Controls

**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.6.1 Validation Approach](#761-validation-approach)
- [7.6.2 Input Validation and Sanitization](#762-input-validation-and-sanitization)

**Implemented controls:** `zod` (`routes/chat.ts`), `sanitize-html@1.4.2` (pinned), `security.sanitizeSecure()` (`routes/saveLoginIp.ts:25`).

**Assessment:** Input validation is applied selectively rather than as a default boundary. The chat endpoint applies zod schema validation; a handful of routes call `security.sanitizeSecure()`. The majority of the 247 routes accept arbitrary request-body structures. `sanitize-html@1.4.2` is pinned to a version that predates multiple bypass CVEs in the current 2.x line.

<a id="validation-approach"></a>
#### 7.6.1 Validation Approach

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - schema validation exists on a small number of routes; most endpoints accept unbounded input structures with no allowlist or type enforcement.

Input validation protects users from malformed or malicious data that reaches server-side logic. `routes/chat.ts` applies `zod` schema validation on the chatbot request body. `security.sanitizeSecure()` at `routes/saveLoginIp.ts:25` wraps `sanitize-html`. The large majority of the 247 Express routes apply no schema, length, or type boundary to request parameters before passing them to Sequelize queries, MarsDB selectors, or file-system operations.

**Security assessment**

There is no centralised validation layer - routes opt in individually, so the coverage is asymmetric. Routes handling user-controlled input that reaches SQL (`routes/login.ts`, `routes/search.ts`), NoSQL (`routes/chat.ts:149`), file paths (`routes/fileServer.ts`, `routes/fileUpload.ts`), and the `eval()` path (`routes/userProfile.ts`, `routes/b2bOrder.ts`) all lack input boundary enforcement before the dangerous sink.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-051](#f-051) — Missing rate limit or input cap on the vast majority of routes enables resource exhaustion and brute-force amplification.
- 🟠 [F-054](#f-054) — No filesize limit on the memory disk image upload (`server.ts:698`); arbitrary-size uploads accepted.
- 🟡 [F-074](#f-074) — YAML deserialization and XML-timeout DoS on `/file-upload` (`routes/fileUpload.ts:109`); no size or schema limit on the XML/YAML parsing path.

<a id="input-validation-and-sanitization"></a>
#### 7.6.2 Input Validation and Sanitization

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - `sanitize-html@1.4.2` is pinned four major versions behind current; selective application leaves most routes unprotected.

`sanitize-html@1.4.2` is referenced in `security.sanitizeSecure()` and applied at `routes/saveLoginIp.ts:25`. The `zod` library validates the chatbot request body at `routes/chat.ts`. Angular's template engine provides output-side escaping by default, but that is disabled at four component sites via `DomSanitizer.bypassSecurityTrustHtml` (see [§7.7](#77-output-encoding-and-rendering-controls)).

**Security assessment**

`sanitize-html@1.4.2` is from 2016; the current version is 2.x with breaking API changes and multiple security fixes across the major versions. Pinning at 1.4.2 means the sanitizer is not receiving CVE patches. The pattern of selective application also means that routes not explicitly listed above accept unsanitized string input, relying on downstream controls (ORM parameterization, Angular escaping) that are themselves defeated elsewhere.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-051](#f-051) — Absent rate limits mean the lack of input caps becomes a DoS amplifier on unvalidated routes.
- 🟠 [F-054](#f-054) — No filesize validation on upload routes allows memory exhaustion.
- 🟡 [F-074](#f-074) — YAML bomb and XML timeout DoS exploit the absence of any deserialization boundary before parsing.

### 7.7 Output Encoding and Rendering Controls

**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.7.1 XSS Output Encoding](#771-xss-output-encoding)

**Implemented controls:** Angular default escaping (partial); `DomSanitizer.bypassSecurityTrustHtml` in 4+ components.

**Assessment:** Angular's template engine escapes interpolated values by default, providing a structural baseline against reflected XSS. However, at least four components deliberately disable this protection by calling `DomSanitizer.bypassSecurityTrustHtml()` and binding the result to `[innerHTML]`, creating direct XSS sinks for attacker-controlled input. No Content-Security-Policy is delivered to contain injected scripts.

<a id="xss-output-encoding"></a>
#### 7.7.1 XSS Output Encoding

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - Angular's default escaping is the primary control, but it is explicitly bypassed at multiple `[innerHTML]` sinks that render attacker-controlled data.

Angular contextual escaping applies when values are bound via `{{ }}` interpolation or `[attr]` bindings without explicit bypass. Four component files call `DomSanitizer.bypassSecurityTrustHtml()` and bind the result to `[innerHTML]`: `search-result.component.ts:143` (the URL query parameter `q`), `about.component.ts:119`, `last-login-ip.component.ts:39`, and additional components in the administration and track-result views. The `q` parameter sink is the widest-exposure instance because it reflects an unauthenticated URL parameter.

The pattern is identical across all affected components:

```ts
// search-result.component.ts:143
this.searchValue = this.sanitizer.bypassSecurityTrustHtml(queryParam)
// template: <span [innerHTML]="searchValue"></span>
```

**Security assessment**

Each `bypassSecurityTrustHtml` call converts a safe Angular binding into a raw HTML sink. At `search-result.component.ts:143`, the URL parameter `q` flows directly to `innerHTML` with no further encoding; a crafted link executes arbitrary JavaScript in the victim session. Combined with JWT storage in `localStorage` and the absence of a Content-Security-Policy, a single injected `<img onerror=...>` payload reads `localStorage.getItem('token')` and beacons it off-origin.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-009](#f-009) — `bypassSecurityTrustHtml` on the search query parameter at `search-result.component.ts:143`; exploitable via URL link.
- 🟠 [F-022](#f-022) — `bypassSecurityTrustHtml` at `about.component.ts:119` reflects server-stored content into `innerHTML`.
- 🟠 [F-056](#f-056) — Unsanitized LLM chatbot output streamed to client (`routes/chat.ts:218`) without server-side content encoding.

### 7.8 Browser and Cross-Origin Controls

**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.8.1 CORS and Security Headers](#781-cors-and-security-headers)

**Implemented controls:** `cors` (wildcard), `helmet.noSniff()`, `helmet.frameguard()`, `featurePolicy`; NO `helmet.contentSecurityPolicy`, NO HSTS.

**Assessment:** Helmet is installed and applies `X-Content-Type-Options: nosniff` and `X-Frame-Options` (via `helmet.frameguard()`). CORS is configured at `server.ts:182` with `app.options('*', cors())` - a wildcard that allows cross-origin requests from any domain. No Content-Security-Policy header is served; no HSTS is configured. The combination of wildcard CORS, no CSP, and JWT in `localStorage` provides the widest possible exfiltration surface for any script injected by XSS.

<a id="cors-and-security-headers"></a>
#### 7.8.1 CORS and Security Headers

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - CORS allows all origins, Content-Security-Policy is absent, and Helmet's XSS filter is commented out.

`server.ts:182-183` configures `app.options('*', cors())` - all origins are permitted without an allowlist. Helmet is applied via `server.ts:186-188`: `helmet.noSniff()` and `helmet.frameguard()` are active. `helmet.contentSecurityPolicy()` is not called. `server.ts:187` contains a commented-out `xssFilter` line, indicating the XSS filter was deliberately removed. HSTS (`Strict-Transport-Security`) is not configured.

**Security assessment**

The absent CSP is the most consequential gap: injected scripts from any XSS sink ([§7.7](#77-output-encoding-and-rendering-controls)) run without restriction and can connect to arbitrary external origins to exfiltrate session tokens. The wildcard CORS policy at `server.ts:182` allows any origin to make authenticated cross-origin requests; combined with Bearer-token authentication, the immediate practical impact is contained, but moving to `HttpOnly` cookies without restricting CORS would enable direct CSRF.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-003](#f-003) — Wildcard CORS at `server.ts:182` allows any origin to issue cross-origin API requests.
- 🟠 [F-028](#f-028) — No Content-Security-Policy delivered; injected scripts have unrestricted execution and outbound connectivity.
- 🟡 [F-058](#f-058) — Wildcard CORS combined with the absence of CSP and HSTS removes all browser-level containment from the XSS blast radius.

### 7.9 Cryptography Secrets and Data Protection

**Verdict:** 🔴 Unsafe

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.9.1 Cryptographic Primitives and Key Management](#791-cryptographic-primitives-and-key-management)

**Implemented controls:** `lib/insecurity.ts:21,41,42`; `lib/utils.ts:86`; Node\.js `crypto` module.

**Assessment:** Cryptographic operations exist across the codebase but every key and secret is either hardcoded in source or uses a broken primitive. The RSA private key at `lib/insecurity.ts:21` is committed to the public repository; the HMAC secret at `lib/insecurity.ts:42` is a hardcoded string; password hashing uses unsalted `MD5` at `lib/insecurity.ts:41` and `models/user.ts:76`; security answer hashing uses `SHA-256` HMAC with the same hardcoded key; PAN data is stored in plaintext. No secrets are loaded from environment variables or a secrets manager at runtime.

<a id="cryptographic-primitives-and-key-management"></a>
#### 7.9.1 Cryptographic Primitives and Key Management

**Status:** 🔴 Unsafe - every cryptographic key and secret is hardcoded in source and multiple operations use broken primitives (`MD5`, `SHA-1` HMAC).

`lib/insecurity.ts` concentrates the cryptographic primitives: `privateKey` (RSA 2048, line 21), `hashSecret` (HMAC key, line 42), `hash()` (`MD5` via `crypto.createHash('md5')`, line 41). Security answers are hashed with `crypto.createHmac('sha256', secret)` at `models/securityAnswer.ts:45` using the same hardcoded HMAC key. Coupon codes use Z85 encoding (`lib/insecurity.ts:106-108`) rather than encryption. The SQLite database file is stored unencrypted at rest (`models/index.ts:41`). Cardholder PAN data is stored as plaintext in the `Cards` model (`models/card.ts:38`).

**Security assessment**

Four independent weaknesses defeat data-at-rest protection:

- `lib/insecurity.ts:21` - RSA private key is a committed string literal, permanently compromised; token forgery requires only a repository clone.
- `lib/insecurity.ts:41` / `models/user.ts:76` - Unsalted `MD5` password hashing has no work factor and is reversible via precomputed lookup tables.
- `lib/insecurity.ts:42` - HMAC key is hardcoded, so coupon-token and deluxe-token MACs can be forged offline.
- `models/card.ts:38` / `models/index.ts:41` - PAN data is unencrypted in a plaintext SQLite file; a path traversal or file-download vulnerability yields raw cardholder data.

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-007](#f-007) — Hardcoded RSA private key at `lib/insecurity.ts:21` enables offline JWT forgery for any user.
- 🟠 [F-018](#f-018) — Hardcoded HMAC secret at `lib/insecurity.ts:42` allows offline coupon and deluxe-token forgery.
- 🟠 [F-032](#f-032) — MD5 unsalted password hashing (`lib/insecurity.ts:41`, `models/user.ts:76`); any credential dump yields crackable hashes immediately.

### 7.10 File Parser and Outbound Request Controls

**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.10.1 File Upload and Path Traversal Prevention](#7101-file-upload-and-path-traversal-prevention)

**Implemented controls:** `file-type` library (`profileImageFileUpload.ts`); `multer`; `path.resolve` (no sanitization in `fileServer.ts:33`).

**Assessment:** File upload and file-serving controls exist but are defeated or incomplete at every path. Magic-byte detection via the `file-type` library is bypassable with polyglot files. The FTP file server at `routes/fileServer.ts:33` uses `path.resolve` without stripping null bytes or enforcing an extension allowlist. Zip extraction in `routes/fileUpload.ts:31` does not validate entry names against the target directory, enabling zip-slip arbitrary writes. XML complaint uploads enable XXE. URL-based profile image upload at `routes/profileImageUrlUpload.ts:24` fetches arbitrary outbound URLs, enabling SSRF.

<a id="file-upload-and-path-traversal-prevention"></a>
#### 7.10.1 File Upload and Path Traversal Prevention

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - magic-byte validation is bypassable, the FTP file server does not strip null bytes, and zip extraction is vulnerable to path traversal.

File uploads flow through two paths: `routes/profileImageFileUpload.ts` uses `multer` with the `file-type` library for MIME validation before writing to disk; `routes/fileUpload.ts` handles complaint ZIP archives, XML files, and YAML documents. The FTP static server at `server.ts:269` serves the `ftp/` directory via `express-serve-index`. `routes/fileServer.ts:33` constructs the served path with `path.resolve('ftp/', file)` from a URL parameter.

**Security assessment**

Four distinct weaknesses cover the file-handling attack surface:

- `routes/fileServer.ts:33` - `path.resolve('ftp/', file)` without null-byte stripping; a `..` or `%00` sequence in the filename escapes the `ftp/` base, enabling traversal reads across the server filesystem.
- `routes/fileUpload.ts:31` - Zip extraction does not validate entry names against the extraction directory; a crafted ZIP with `../../` entry names writes to arbitrary server paths (zip-slip).
- `routes/fileUpload.ts:76` - XML parsing via `libxmljs2` with `noent: true`; an XML file including an external entity reads local files and returns their content in error responses (XXE).
- `routes/profileImageUrlUpload.ts:24` - Fetches the caller-supplied URL without allowlisting; internal metadata endpoints (`http://169.254.169.254/`) are reachable (SSRF).

**Relevant findings**

- 🔴 [F-013](#f-013) — Null-byte path traversal in the FTP file server (`routes/fileServer.ts:33`).
- 🟠 [F-027](#f-027) — Zip-slip path traversal via complaint ZIP extraction (`routes/fileUpload.ts:31`).
- 🟠 [F-030](#f-030) — XXE file disclosure via XML complaint upload with `noent:true` (`routes/fileUpload.ts:76`).

### 7.11 Operations Runtime and Supply Chain Controls

**Verdict:** 🟠 Weak

<!-- The line below is mechanically derived from the controls table — LLM must not re-author it. -->
**Controls covered:**

- [7.11.1 CI/CD Pipeline Security](#7111-cicd-pipeline-security)
- [7.11.2 Container and Runtime Hardening](#7112-container-and-runtime-hardening)
- [7.11.3 Dependency Management](#7113-dependency-management)
- [7.11.4 Security Logging and Audit Trail](#7114-security-logging-and-audit-trail)

**Implemented controls:** `.github/workflows/ci.yml` (unpinned), Dockerfile (`npm` without `--ignore-scripts`), `.cyclonedx` SBOM (present); Dockerfile: distroless base, UID 65532, multi-stage build; `package.json` pinned versions; no Dependabot; SBOM present; `winston@3.x` (console transport), `morgan` HTTP logging; no SIEM integration observed.

**Assessment:** The container runtime posture is the single strongest positive control: a distroless base image, a dedicated non-root UID (65532), and a multi-stage build prevent most post-exploitation persistence paths. All other supply-chain and operational controls are weak or absent. Six workflow files lack top-level `permissions` blocks, third-party actions are unpinned, Dependabot covers only one ecosystem, and critical security events (authentication failures, privilege changes, admin operations) are not emitted as structured, append-only audit events.

<a id="cicd-pipeline-security"></a>
#### 7.11.1 CI/CD Pipeline Security

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - six workflow files lack `permissions` blocks, third-party actions are unpinned, and `npm install` in the Dockerfile runs postinstall scripts without restriction.

`.github/workflows/ci.yml`, `release.yml`, `rebase.yml`, `zap_scan.yml`, `stale.yml`, and `update-news-www.yml` all omit a top-level `permissions:` block, defaulting to `contents: write` and granting any injected step broad repository access. Third-party actions (`coverallsapp/github-action`, `docker/build-push-action`, `calibreapp/image-actions`) are referenced by mutable branch tags rather than immutable commit SHAs. `Dockerfile:5` runs `npm install` without `--ignore-scripts`, allowing any postinstall hook in the dependency tree to execute during the image build.

**Security assessment**

Three independent pipeline weaknesses:

- Mutable action references (`@main`, `@v1`) at `.github/workflows/ci.yml:188`, `codeql-analysis.yml:22`, and `image_actions.yml:30` allow a compromised upstream action repository to inject malicious steps into the build without a pull request.
- Missing `permissions:` blocks at `.github/workflows/ci.yml:1` (and five others) means a supply-chain injection executes with `contents: write`, enabling repository modification.
- `Dockerfile:5` runs postinstall hooks from 66 production dependencies - a compromised transitive package can exfiltrate build secrets or inject backdoors during the image build.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-024](#f-024) — Mutable-ref action (`image_actions.yml:33`) executes with the repo token; tag can be redirected.
- 🟠 [F-025](#f-025) — `npm install` without `--ignore-scripts` in `Dockerfile:5` enables postinstall hook execution.
- 🟠 [F-026](#f-026) — Unpinned third-party CI actions at `ci.yml:188` allow silent malicious step injection.

<a id="container-and-runtime-hardening"></a>
#### 7.11.2 Container and Runtime Hardening

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - distroless base image and non-root UID are implemented; Dockerfile base images are not digest-pinned.

The production `Dockerfile` uses a multi-stage build: the builder stage uses `node:24` and the runtime stage is `gcr.io/distroless/nodejs24-debian13` running as UID 65532. The distroless base excludes a shell, package manager, and most OS utilities, reducing the post-exploitation attack surface. The container has no writeable package repository.

**Security assessment**

The runtime hardening is the application's strongest positive control. The distroless image and non-root UID together prevent the most common post-exploitation escalation paths (apt-get package install, shell invocation). The one gap is that `Dockerfile:1` references the base image by mutable tag (`node:24`) rather than a `@sha256:<digest>` pin - a silently-updated upstream image introduces unreviewed changes to the build.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-024](#f-024) — Dockerfile base image (`Dockerfile:1`) is referenced by tag, not SHA digest; silent upstream changes bypass the build review process.
- 🟠 [F-025](#f-025) — Postinstall hooks in the `npm install` build step execute inside the image build context with access to build secrets.
- 🟠 [F-026](#f-026) — Unpinned actions that have `contents: write` could modify the Dockerfile itself in a compromised pipeline run.

<a id="dependency-management"></a>
#### 7.11.3 Dependency Management

**Status:** 🟠 Weak - direct dependencies are version-pinned in `package.json` but pinned to old versions with known vulnerabilities; Dependabot is not configured for the npm ecosystem.

`package.json` pins critical security-relevant packages to exact versions: `express-jwt@0.1.3`, `jsonwebtoken@0.4.0`, `sanitize-html@1.4.2`, `colors@1.4.0`. These pins prevent accidental auto-update but also prevent security patches from being applied. The SBOM (`.cyclonedx`) is present. `.github/dependabot.yml` is present but covers only one ecosystem (not npm).

**Security assessment**

Version pinning serves as a supply-chain anchor but is used here to hold deliberately-vulnerable library versions in place. `express-jwt@0.1.3` and `jsonwebtoken@0.4.0` are over a decade behind current releases and carry multiple CVEs. The absence of npm Dependabot configuration means no automated PRs would surface new advisories even if the intentional-vuln pins were relaxed.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-024](#f-024) — Unpinned base images and action references are the supply-chain complement to pinned but outdated npm packages.
- 🟠 [F-025](#f-025) — `npm install` with postinstall hooks in the build stage is especially risky when transitive dependencies include unmaintained packages (`marsdb@^0.6.11`, `colors@1.4.0`).
- 🟠 [F-026](#f-026) — Dependabot absence (`github/dependabot.yml:1`) means no automated advisory coverage for the npm ecosystem.

<a id="security-logging-and-audit-trail"></a>
#### 7.11.4 Security Logging and Audit Trail

**Status:** 🟡 Partial - HTTP access logging is present via `morgan`; structured security-event logging for authentication, authorization, and admin operations is absent.

`winston@3.x` provides application-level logging to the console transport. `morgan` HTTP access logging records request method, path, status, and response time. Neither library is configured to emit structured, append-only audit events for security-relevant state changes.

**Security assessment**

The logging posture captures the HTTP access tier but misses the security-event tier. Authentication failures, account lockouts, admin operations, privilege changes, and payment events emit no structured events. `routes/login.ts:32`, `routes/b2bOrder.ts:16`, `routes/chat.ts:181`, `routes/memory.ts:10`, `routes/nftMint.ts:44`, `lib/startup/registerWebsocketEvents.ts:33`, and `models/index.ts:42` are all identified as missing security event logging. Without these events, post-incident forensics and real-time anomaly detection are unavailable.

**Relevant findings**

- 🟠 [F-024](#f-024) — CI pipeline security events (build failures, permission escalations) are also not audited.
- 🟠 [F-025](#f-025) — Supply-chain compromise via postinstall hooks would leave no security audit trace.
- 🟠 [F-026](#f-026) — Compromised CI action execution would not trigger a security event in the absence of audit logging.

### 7.12 Real-time and Not Applicable Controls

<!-- §7.12 LOCKED — mechanically derived from absence of real-time findings. Renderer must not rewrite the line below. -->
_Not applicable - no real-time / WebSocket findings routed to this category, and no AI/LLM, GraphQL, or gRPC surfaces detected by the recon scan. Controls catalogued elsewhere (container hardening, dependency determinism) are covered in their primary [§7](#7-security-architecture) sections._

### 7.13 Defense-in-Depth Summary

**Verdict:** 🔴 Unsafe

The application's strongest positive controls are at the runtime layer: the distroless container base image and UID 65532 effectively eliminate the post-exploitation shell and package-install paths available to an attacker who achieves code execution. Angular's default template escaping provides structural XSS mitigation on the majority of rendering surfaces. The `RS256` algorithm choice and the presence of `express-jwt` middleware establish the correct _shape_ of a session-token control, and the SBOM provides a baseline dependency inventory for future vulnerability scanning.

The defense-in-depth model collapses at the application layer because each control category is defeated at its root: the JWT signing key is committed to the public repository, the SQL login and search paths concatenate user input without parameterization, the role claim is read from a client-forgeable token, HMAC and password secrets use broken primitives, and the `localStorage` token placement makes XSS immediately yield a full session credential. Restoring layered defense requires four boundary repairs in parallel: rotate and externalize all signing secrets (key store, not source code); parameterize the login and search queries; add a Content-Security-Policy and move the session token to an `HttpOnly Secure SameSite=Strict` cookie; and replace `MD5`/SHA-1 HMAC hashing with a modern KDF (bcrypt or Argon2id) for passwords and HMAC-SHA-256 with a runtime-injected secret for coupon tokens.

### 🔴 Critical (11)

<a id="t-005"></a><a id="f-005"></a>
#### F-005 · Improper Authentication

**Severity:** 🔴 Critical  ·  **Component:** [C-02](#c-02) - Angular SPA Frontend  ·  **Location:** `frontend/src/app/oauth/oauth.component.ts:30`

**Issue:** `oauth.component.ts` implements an OAuth implicit flow: `access_token` is parsed from the URL fragment (parseRedirectUrlParams, line 70-79, consumed at line 28) with no PKCE, no `state`/`nonce` validation, and no verification of the redirect parameters. Worse, line 30 and line 46 derive the account password deterministically as `btoa(profile.email reversed)`.

Because that derivation is a pure function of the email, anyone who knows a victim's email can reconstruct their password and authenticate through the normal `/rest/user/login` endpoint - the OAuth convenience flow silently creates a parallel, guessable credential that bypasses the OAuth provider entirely. The access token also rides in the URL fragment, exposing it to browser history, referrer leakage, and any XSS on the page.

Any party knowing a target email can compute the password and fully impersonate the account, defeating OAuth.

**Root cause:** Authentication can be circumvented or forged because credentials, signing keys, or password hashes are weak, missing, or exposed.

**Evidence:** ✓ verified - `oauth.component.ts:30`/46 set the user's password to btoa(reversed email); line 28/71 consume an implicit-flow access_token from the URL fragment without PKCE/state.

```typescript
// frontend/src/app/oauth/oauth.component.ts:30
  ngOnInit (): void {
    this.userService.oauthLogin(this.parseRedirectUrlParams().access_token).subscribe({
      next: (profile: any) => {
        const password = btoa(profile.email.split('').reverse().join(''))
        this.userService.save({ email: profile.email, password, passwordRepeat: password }).subscribe({
          next: () => {
            this.login(profile)
```

**Fix:** Strengthen authentication: enforce a vetted JWT verifier with explicit algorithm, MFA where appropriate → ❶ [M-005](#m-005) — Replace the derived OAuth password with a server-generated secret and adopt code flow with PKCE

**Classification:** OAuth / OIDC Misconfiguration · [CWE-287](https://cwe.mitre.org/data/definitions/287.html) · [OWASP A07:2021](https://owasp.org/Top10/A07_2021/)

<a id="t-006"></a><a id="f-006"></a>
#### F-006 · SQL Injection

**Severity:** 🔴 Critical  ·  **Component:** [C-08](#c-08) - Authentication & Session Surface  ·  **Location:** `routes/login.ts:34`

**Issue:** `routes/login.ts:34` builds the authentication query by interpolating `req.body.email` directly into a raw SQL string passed to `models.sequelize.query()`: SELECT * FROM Users WHERE email = '\${`req.body.email`}' AND password = '\${`security.hash`(...)}' AND deletedAt IS NULL. Submitting email = ' OR true-- to POST `/rest/user/login` comments out the password and deletedAt clauses; the query returns the first Users row, which is the seeded admin@<domain> account, and `afterLogin()` (`routes/login.ts:48`) issues a valid admin JWT.

The plain:true / model option returns a single mapped user, so the attacker is logged in as that user. Pre-authentication login as any user (notably admin) and, depending on DB, arbitrary read of the Users table via UNION-style payloads.

**Root cause:** User input flows into a server-side interpreter (SQL, NoSQL, XML, YAML, LDAP, OS shell) without parameterization or schema validation.

**Evidence:** ✓ verified - `req.body.email` is concatenated into a raw SQL template literal at `routes/login.ts:34` with no parameterisation or escaping.

```typescript
// routes/login.ts:34

  return (req: Request, res: Response, next: NextFunction) => {
    verifyPreLoginChallenges(req) // vuln-code-snippet hide-line
    models.sequelize.query(`SELECT * FROM Users WHERE email = '${req.body.email || ''}' AND password = '${security.hash(req.body.password || '')}' AND deletedAt IS NULL`, { model: UserModel, plain: tr
      .then((authenticatedUser) => { // vuln-code-snippet neutral-line loginAdminChallenge loginBenderChallenge loginJimChallenge
        const user = utils.queryResultToJson(authenticatedUser)
        if (user.data?.id && user.data.totpSecret !== '') {
```

**Fix:** Switch all SQL execution to parameterised queries or ORM-bound parameters → ❶ [M-006](#m-006) — Replace the raw login query with a parameterized Sequelize query

**Classification:** Injection · [CWE-89](https://cwe.mitre.org/data/definitions/89.html) · [OWASP A03:2021](https://owasp.org/Top10/A03_2021/)

<a id="t-007"></a><a id="f-007"></a>
#### F-007 · Hardcoded Cryptographic Key

**Severity:** 🔴 Critical  ·  **Component:** [C-08](#c-08) - Authentication & Session Surface  ·  **Location:** `lib/insecurity.ts:21`

**Issue:** The 2048-bit RSA private key used to sign every session JWT is a string literal, committed to the public OWASP Juice Shop repository. `authorize()` signs tokens with this key (`jwt.sign`(user, privateKey, {algorithm: 'RS256'})).

Anyone who clones the repo can mint a token the server accepts for any user: set `data.id` and `data.role`='admin', sign with the committed key, and present it as a Bearer token. The server's `verify()` checks the signature against `encryptionkeys/jwt.pub`, which is the matching public key - so the forged token validates.

Full authentication bypass and impersonation of any account, including the seeded admin, with no credential knowledge.

**Root cause:** Authentication can be circumvented or forged because credentials, signing keys, or password hashes are weak, missing, or exposed.

**Evidence:** ✓ verified - privateKey is a literal RSA key at `lib/insecurity.ts:21` and is the only signing secret, consumed by `authorize()` at line 54.

**Fix:** Move the cryptographic key out of source control into a managed secret store and rotate it → ❶ [M-007](#m-007) — Move the JWT signing key out of source into a runtime secret store and rotate it

**Classification:** Cryptographic Failures · [CWE-321](https://cwe.mitre.org/data/definitions/321.html) · [OWASP A02:2021](https://owasp.org/Top10/A02_2021/)

<a id="t-008"></a><a id="f-008"></a>
#### F-008 · Improper Verification of Cryptographic Signature

**Severity:** 🔴 Critical  ·  **Component:** [C-08](#c-08) - Authentication & Session Surface  ·  **Location:** `lib/insecurity.ts:55`

**Instances (6):** 🔴 `lib/insecurity.ts:55`, 🟠 `lib/insecurity.ts:52`, 🟠 `lib/insecurity.ts:53`, 🟠 `lib/insecurity.ts:56`, 🔴 `lib/insecurity.ts:189`, 🔴 `routes/verify.ts:120`

**Issue:** `verify()` calls jws.verify(token, publicKey) with no algorithm allowlist, and `updateAuthenticatedUsers()` calls jwt.verify(token, publicKey, ...) without an algorithms option. Because no algorithm is pinned, an attacker can submit a token with the header alg set to 'none' (no signature required) or attempt `RS256`-to-`HS256` confusion where the public key in `encryptionkeys/jwt.pub` is treated as an HMAC secret.

With `jsonwebtoken@0.4.0` (an ancient version with known alg-confusion behaviour), a crafted unsigned token can be accepted, letting the attacker forge arbitrary identity claims without the private key. Token forgery without the signing key, yielding authentication bypass and arbitrary-user impersonation.

**Root cause:** Authentication can be circumvented or forged because credentials, signing keys, or password hashes are weak, missing, or exposed.

**Evidence:** ✓ verified - Neither `verify()` (line 55) nor `jwt.verify()` (line 189) pins an algorithm, so the verifier honours the attacker-controlled header alg field.

```typescript
// lib/insecurity.ts:55
export const isAuthorized = () => expressJwt(({ secret: publicKey }) as any)
export const denyAll = () => expressJwt({ secret: '' + Math.random() } as any)
export const authorize = (user = {}) => jwt.sign(user, privateKey, { expiresIn: '6h', algorithm: 'RS256' })
export const verify = (token: string) => token ? (jws.verify as ((token: string, secret: string) => boolean))(token, publicKey) : false
export const decode = (token: string) => { return jws.decode(token)?.payload }

export const sanitizeHtml = (html: string) => sanitizeHtmlLib(html)
```

**Fix:** Pin the signature algorithm explicitly and reject `alg:none` and unknown algorithms → ❶ [M-008](#m-008) — Pin RS256 as the only accepted algorithm in every JWT verification call

**Classification:** Broken Authentication · [CWE-347](https://cwe.mitre.org/data/definitions/347.html) · [OWASP A07:2021](https://owasp.org/Top10/A07_2021/)

<a id="t-009"></a><a id="f-009"></a>
#### F-009 · Cross-Site Scripting

**Severity:** 🔴 Critical  ·  **Component:** [C-02](#c-02) - Angular SPA Frontend  ·  **Location:** `frontend/src/app/search-result/search-result.component.ts:143`

**Instances (3):** 🔴 `frontend/src/app/search-result/search-result.component.ts:143`, 🔴 `frontend/src/app/about/about.component.ts:119`, 🟠 `frontend/src/app/last-login-ip/last-login-ip.component.ts:39`

**Issue:** `search-result.component.ts:143` takes the raw `q` query parameter (`this.route.snapshot.queryParams.q`) and passes it directly through `DomSanitizer.bypassSecurityTrustHtml`, then binds the result to `[innerHTML]="searchValue"` at `search-result.component.html`:11. Angular's built-in contextual escaping is explicitly turned off by the bypass call, so a link such as `/#/search?q=<img src=x onerror=alert(document.cookie)>` executes attacker JavaScript in the victim's session.

Because the JWT lives in `localStorage` (read by the same origin) and there is no Content-Security-Policy, the injected script can read `localStorage.getItem('token')` and POST it to an attacker host - full account takeover from a single crafted URL. Arbitrary script execution in the victim origin, leading to JWT theft from localStorage and account takeover.

**Root cause:** Attacker-controlled content is rendered in the victim's browser without sanitization; combined with session tokens held in JavaScript-readable storage, any payload yields immediate account takeover.

**Evidence:** ✓ verified - `search-result.component.ts:143` wraps the attacker-controlled `q` param in bypassSecurityTrustHtml and renders it via [innerHTML].

```typescript
// frontend/src/app/search-result/search-result.component.ts:143
        this.io.socket().emit('verifyLocalXssChallenge', queryParam)
      }) // vuln-code-snippet hide-end
      this.dataSource.filter = queryParam.toLowerCase()
      this.searchValue = this.sanitizer.bypassSecurityTrustHtml(queryParam) // vuln-code-snippet vuln-line localXssChallenge xssBonusChallenge
      if (this.gridDataSourceSubscription) {
        this.gridDataSourceSubscription.unsubscribe()
      }
```

**Fix:** Output-encode untrusted strings at every sink and remove all `bypassSecurityTrustHtml` calls → ❶ [M-009](#m-009) — Remove bypassSecurityTrustHtml from the search reflection and render the query as text

**Classification:** Cross-Site Scripting (XSS) · [CWE-79](https://cwe.mitre.org/data/definitions/79.html) · [OWASP A03:2021](https://owasp.org/Top10/A03_2021/)

<a id="t-010"></a><a id="f-010"></a>
#### F-010 · Insecure Direct Object Reference (IDOR)

**Severity:** 🔴 Critical  ·  **Component:** [C-06](#c-06) - File Server and Upload Handler  ·  **Location:** `routes/memory.ts:15`

**Instances (22):** 🟠 `server.ts:346`, 🔴 `routes/memory.ts:15`, 🟡 `lib/startup/registerWebsocketEvents.ts:33`, 🔴 `routes/address.ts:11`, 🔴 `routes/address.ts:18`, 🔴 `routes/address.ts:29`, 🟠 `routes/basketItems.ts:68`, 🔴 `routes/dataExport.ts:26` … (+14 more)

**Issue:** Server-side authorization MUST derive the resource owner from the authenticated session (`req.user` / `req.session` / `req.auth`), never from attacker-controlled request data. Trusting `req.body.UserId` etc. enables horizontal privilege escalation across all authenticated tenants.

**Root cause:** Authorization checks are absent or bypassable, allowing horizontal and vertical privilege jumps from a self-registered or low-rights account. Includes mass-assignment of privileged attributes.

**Evidence:** ✓ verified - An object-identity parameter is trusted from the request without server-side ownership check.

```typescript
// routes/memory.ts:15
    const record = {
      caption: req.body.caption,
      imagePath: 'assets/public/images/uploads/' + req.file?.filename,
      UserId: req.body.UserId
    }
    const memory = await MemoryModel.create(record)
    res.status(200).json({ status: 'success', data: memory })
```

**Fix:** Tie every object lookup to the requesting user's identity and reject cross-tenant references → ❶ [M-010](#m-010) — Replace req.body.UserId/userId/ownerId with req.user.id (or equivalent session-derived identity) in every WHERE/filter clause.

**Classification:** Broken Access Control · [CWE-639](https://cwe.mitre.org/data/definitions/639.html) · [OWASP A01:2021](https://owasp.org/Top10/A01_2021/)

<a id="t-011"></a><a id="f-011"></a>
#### F-011 · SQL Injection

**Severity:** 🔴 Critical  ·  **Component:** [C-01](#c-01) - Express REST API Backend  ·  **Location:** `routes/search.ts:23`

**Issue:** `searchProducts()` interpolates the unauthenticated `req.query.q` into SELECT * FROM Products WHERE ((name LIKE '%\${criteria}%' OR description LIKE '%\${criteria}%') ...). The 200-char truncation at line 22 does not prevent injection.

An attacker sends q=qwert')) UNION SELECT id,email,password,...FROM Users-- to merge the Users table (including `MD5` password hashes) into the product results returned by `res.json` at line 68. The schema itself is also readable via the adjacent SELECT sql FROM sqlite_master query at line 47.

Unauthenticated exfiltration of the entire Users table - emails and crackable `MD5` password hashes - plus full database schema disclosure.

**Root cause:** User input flows into a server-side interpreter (SQL, NoSQL, XML, YAML, LDAP, OS shell) without parameterization or schema validation.

**Evidence:** ◌ ambiguous - `models.sequelize.query` at `routes/search.ts:23` embeds the raw criteria string from `req.query.q` with no binding, on a publicly reachable GET `/rest/products/search` route.

